            }
            return "blank";
        }

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="19f1ca2129ab49f898fc782da7d8f567">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '3553bb6e-36f1-44b4-bfc4-60d1fe57b1ad'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d7751247" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A series of Cobalt-Based Prussian blue analogues (CoM-PBAs) as anode materials for SIBs are designed by a universal low-energy co-precipitation approach through the rational incorporation of 3<i>d</i> transition metals. Among them, the CoV-PBA (CV) anode exhibits remarkable sodium-ion storage performance, which is attributed to the synergistic modulation of its electronic structure through π-electron regulation induced by vanadium incorporation.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b33a8846-ec87-4f21-a39c-3d239dde3054/anie202423356-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b33a8846-ec87-4f21-a39c-3d239dde3054/anie202423356-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7f6c7afc-d43c-455a-979d-19a3386ddc48/anie202423356-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b33a8846-ec87-4f21-a39c-3d239dde3054/anie202423356-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d7751250" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Understanding the relationship between structure regulation and electrochemical performance is key to developing efficient and sustainable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) materials. Herein, seven Cobalt-M-based (M=V, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Zn) Prussian blue analogues (CoM-PBAs) are designed as anodes for SIBs <i>via</i> a universal low-energy co-precipitation approach with the strategic inclusion of 3d transition metals. Density Functional Theory (DFT) simulation and experimental validation reveal that a moderate <i>p</i>-band center of cyanide linkages (−CN−) is more favorable for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and diffusion, while the <i>d</i>-band center of metal cations is linearly related to electrode stability. Among seven CoM-based PBAs, CoV-PBAs possess the best sodium-ion adsorption/diffusion kinetics and overall cycling performance, including high specific capacity (565 mAh/g at 0.1 A/g), cycling stability (over 15000 cycles with 97.7 % capacity retention), and superior rate capability (174.7 mAh/g at 30 A/g). <i>In situ</i>/<i>ex situ</i> techniques further demonstrate that the π-electron regulation by V introduction enhances the reversibility and kinetics of redox reactions. Moreover, the study identified the “<i>p</i>-band center” and “<i>d</i>-band center” may serve as key descriptors for quantifying the capability and stability of other-type bimetal Co-based anodes (oxides, phosphides, sulfides, and selenides) with similar theoretical capacity, offering a potentially transformative approach for selecting practical SIB electrode materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423356-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423356-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) possess significant potential for grid-scale energy storage applications owing to their lower costs than lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and higher energy density than traditional lead-acid batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0001_R_d7751237e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> However, a significant challenge in SIB applications arises from the large ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, which limits the availability of high-capacity and reversible anode materials.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0006_R_d7751237e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">2</a></sup></span> Although various anode materials,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0007_R_d7751237e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> including hard carbon (HC),<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0010_R_d7751237e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> conversion-type materials,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0014_R_d7751237e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> and alloy-type materials,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0017_R_d7751237e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">6</a></sup></span> have been explored, their synthesis typically involves energy-intensive processes such as high-temperature annealing and solvothermal methods. Moreover, the unclear relationship between the electronic structure of these anodes and their electrochemical performance complicates the development of efficient materials. Consequently, developing the SIBs anode materials with green synthesis methods and universal design principles is important.</p>
         
         <p>Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) have attracted increasing attention as potential cathode materials for SIBs<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0018_R_d7751237e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> because of their robust open lattice structure, tunable chemical composition, and low-energy synthesis methods.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0021_R_d7751237e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> However, their electrochemical properties as anodes remain inadequately understood. Cobalt PBA (Co<sub>3</sub>[Co(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub>, CoHCC) emerges as a promising candidate for insertion anodes in non-aqueous alkali-metal-ion batteries (AMIBs), as its low-spin Co<sup>3+</sup> configuration requires significant energy for electron excitation, which results in a highly negative reduction potential.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0024_R_d7751237e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, the monometallic CoHCC exhibits poor electronic conductivity and alkali-metal-ion adsorption and diffusion kinetics, leading to limited rate capability (≤ 30 A/g) and cycling stability (≤ 5000 cycles).<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0024_R_d7751237e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> Finding a proper way to enhance the sodium storage performance of Co-based PBA anodes is critical.</p>
         
         <p>It is widely accepted that modulating the electronic structure of anode materials significantly influences their sodium ion storage kinetics and cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0028_R_d7751237e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> Non-metallic <i>p</i>-electronic structures affect Na<sup>+</sup> ion adsorption and transport,<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0033_R_d7751237e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10e - Adv. Mater.">10e</a>, <a href="#anie202423356-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0036_R_d7751237e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10h - Chin. Chem. Lett.">10h</a></sup></span> while the <i>d</i>-electronic structure of metallic atoms determines electrochemical reversibility.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0016_R_d7751237e641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5b - Nat. Commun.">5b</a></sup></span> For example, <i>Yin</i> et al. demonstrated that optimizing the <i>p</i>-band center of HC using a ZnO-assisted bulk etching method enhances the charge-storage kinetics of the HC anode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0033_R_d7751237e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10e - Adv. Mater.">10e</a></sup></span> Similarly, <i>Jiang</i> et al. showed that adjusting the <i>d</i>-band center allows MoSe<sub>2</sub> to achieve highly reversible sodium ion storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0016_R_d7751237e658" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5b - Nat. Commun.">5b</a></sup></span> To the best of our survey, there are few systematic reports on simultaneously regulating the <i>p</i>-band and <i>d</i>-band structures in SIB anodes by incorporating <i>3d</i> transition metals up to date, although this approach is vital for developing high-efficiency anodes. In addition, the criteria and descriptors for selecting optimal transition metal require further investigation. As a simple model of an insertion anode, in Co-based PBAs, the metal-ligand interactions significantly influence electrochemical performance, making incorporating <i>3d</i> transition metal elements a strategic approach to optimize both ligand <i>p</i>-band and metal <i>d</i>-band electron structures. Such optimization can enhance sodium storage performance and facilitate a deeper understanding of how element incorporation affects electrochemical behavior. Furthermore, this research is meaningful in identifying suitable descriptors and universal design principles for effective anode materials.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, the Density functional theory (DFT) computational screening and experimental analysis results revealed the following aspects, a). For seven Co−M-based PBA (V, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, and Zn), a moderate <i>p</i>-band center of cyanide linkages (−CN−) with balanced Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) and diffusion energy (<i>E<sub>d</sub></i>) would be more favorable for the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and diffusion. b). The <i>d</i>-band center of the metal cations and the integrated crystal orbital Hamilton population (ICOHP) of the TM−N band are linearly correlated with the stability of these PBAs. Among these PBAs, it is found that CoV-PBAs possess the best sodium-ion adsorption/diffusion kinetics and cycling performance. Interestingly, all seven Co−M-based PBAs can be synthesized using a low-energy Co-precipitation method. <i>In situ</i>/<i>ex situ</i> techniques reveal that the π-electron regulation by V introduction leads to a synergistic modulation of the electronic structure of CoV-PBA, enhancing the reversibility and kinetics of redox reactions. Consequently, the CoV-PBA anode delivers remarkable sodium-ion storage performance which outputs high specific capacity (~565 mAh/g at 0.1 A/g), remarkable cycling stability (over 15000 cycles with ~97.7 % capacity retention), and superior rate capability (~174.7 mAh/g at 30 A/g). Moreover, the <i>“p</i>-band center” and “<i>d</i>-band center” may serve as effective descriptors for quantifying the capability and stability of other Co-based bimetal anodes (oxides, phosphides, sulfides, selenides) with similar theoretical capacities. This discovery allows one, in principle, to reduce the number of relevant parameters despite the complex structure of the anode materials, and then greatly expand the screening of anode materials selection for practical SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423356-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423356-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The evolution of the electronic structures upon the tuning of different metals (V, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Zn, Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0001">1a</a>) was first verified by DFT calculations. In the corresponding density of states (DOS) curve (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0001">1b</a> and S1, Supporting Information), compared to monometallic PBA (Co<sub>3</sub>[Co(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub>, CoHCC), bimetal Co-based PBAs exhibit high electron density at the fermi level, indicating the higher electronic conductivity, which is contributed by more pronounced electron transfer between half Co's and half alien metal's electron states. The improved electronic conductivity is beneficial to the Na-storage reaction kinetics during cycling. It is noteworthy that, compared to other metals, vanadium exhibits fewer <i>t<sub>2g</sub></i> orbital occupations in its outermost shell. This characteristic enhances the π-donation between vanadium and nitrogen, consequently leading to superior electrical conductivity in cobalt-vanadium (CoV)-PBAs. As a reference for the chemical potential of electrons, the Fermi level can indicate the strength of a material‘s oxidation/reduction ability. Among all seven Prussian blue materials, Co−V PBA features the Fermi level of −0.341 eV closest to the vacuum level, enabling it to exhibit the lowest average voltage of 0.401 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) when used as anode for SIBs, which is beneficial to improve the energy density of the full battery (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0001">1c</a>). As shown in Figures <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0001">1d</a>, <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0001">e</a>, and S2, the position of the <i>p</i>-band center can be an effective descriptor with the magnitudes of the adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) and diffusion energy (<i>E<sub>d</sub></i>). Specifically, a displacement of the <i>p</i>-band center further away from (or nearer to) the Fermi level is associated with decreased (or increased) adsorption strength and a reduction (or augmentation) in the diffusion barrier (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0001">1g</a>). Co−V PBA stands out among the seven Co-based PBA materials due to its moderate <i>p-</i>band center (−1.72 eV, Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0001">1f</a>), which results in a balanced <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> (−2.019 eV) and <i>E<sub>d</sub></i> (0.48 eV), leading to improved Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and diffusion properties. As evidenced by the charge density difference analyses, the charge gain/loss region near the Na<sup>+</sup> presents a homogeneous distribution, mainly due to the moderate adsorption capacity of the inner sites of CoV-PBA for Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure S3). The <i>d-</i>band center and Crystal Orbital Hamilton Population (COHP) have been calculated to evaluate the stability of the above Co-based PBAs. Notably, the CoV-PBA (Co−V PBA) exhibits the highest <i>d</i>-band center value (−0.745 eV), suggesting the metal centers (Co and V) in this material possessed the greatest adsorption capability towards the surrounding cyano (−C≡N−) ligand groups. Furthermore, the -ICOHP value is found to have a linear correlation with the <i>d</i>-band, and the CoV-PBA displays the most positive -ICOHP value (1.52), indicative of the strongest metal-nitrogen (M−N) bond strength and enhanced thermodynamic stability (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0001">1f</a>, <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0001">h</a> and Figure S4). Taken together, these findings strongly suggest that the CoV-PBA holds great promise as a highly stable anode material for SIBs.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423356-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fcdaf11d-6947-4bae-8269-1ac0964ebeeb/anie202423356-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fcdaf11d-6947-4bae-8269-1ac0964ebeeb/anie202423356-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/89bfa4b5-b246-420c-ac69-33ab6436e294/anie202423356-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fcdaf11d-6947-4bae-8269-1ac0964ebeeb/anie202423356-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423356-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423356" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Theoretical insights of the materials design principle. (a) Schematic illustrations of the structures, (b) the total density of states, and (c) the Fermi level and the average potential of the Co-based PBAs. (d) A comparison of the migration energy barriers of the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, (e) the relationship between the <i>p</i>-band center, the <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> (Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energies), and <i>E<sub>d</sub></i> (the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier energies) of various Co-based PBAs, (f) the <i>d</i>-band center and -ICOHP values of Co-based PBAs. Schematic representation of the relationships between (g) <i>p</i>-band centers with Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics, and (h) <i>d</i>-band centers with stability.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Seven cobalt-based PBA samples were synthesized using a conventional coprecipitation process (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">2a</a>). As shown in scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images, CoV-PBA, CoMn-PBA, CoFe-PBA, CoCo-PBA, CoNi-PBA, CoCu-PBA, and CoZn-PBA exhibit regular cubic shape with particle sizes of ~~290, ~1350, ~430 nm, ~500, ~150, ~480 and ~900 nm, respectively (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">2b</a>–<a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">f</a>, S5). All the element mapping analysis results demonstrate the homogeneous distribution of the corresponding metal elements in the PBA face-centered cubic framework (Figure S6). The stoichiometric compositions of the above seven Co-based PBA samples were determined by the inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometer (ICP-OES) (Table S1, Supporting Information). The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns (Figure S7), and FTIR spectra (Figure S8) results confirm that the seven materials exhibit high purity and good crystallinity. Notably, the positions of the diffraction peaks in the XRD results of the seven samples mentioned above are consistent with the changing trend of their ionic radius (Figure S9). The Na-storage competencies of the Co-based PBA electrodes were evaluated to confirm the feasibility of using “<i>p</i>-band center, <i>d</i>-band center” as descriptors to predict the sodium storage capacity and stability of bimetallic Co-based PBA anodes. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) (Figure S10a) simulating the distribution of relaxation time (DRT) technology is utilized to probe the interfacial charge transfer kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0038_R_d7751237e849" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> The CoV-PBA features the smallest peak at 10<sup>−5</sup> s representing the lowest system resistance (<i>R<sub>0</sub></i>), suggesting the highest intrinsic electronic conductivity of CoV-PBA, corresponding to the results of DOS (Figure S10b). In addition, the results of the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) demonstrate that Co−V PBAs possess moderate Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub><i>Na+</i></sub>) of 10<sup>−10</sup> to 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup>/s, aligning with the results of the theoretical simulation (Figure S10c). The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curve shows that CoV-PBA delivers the highest charge-specific capacity (521.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A/g) with the smallest polarization voltage of 0.84 V. In addition, the capacity of other Co-based PBA anodes with the structure regulation by different metals including CoFe-PBA (247.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), CoCo-PBA (300.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), CoNi-PBA (395 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), CoCu-PBA (211 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and CoZn-PBA (281.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) demonstrating consistency with the <i>p</i>-band center (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">2g</a>). Figure S11 details the cycle performance over 60 cycles at a consistent current density (0.2 A/g) of the above-mentioned Co-based anodes. Among all samples, the CoV-PBA electrode showcases the lowest capacity decay rate of 0.002/cycle, affirming its stability and robustness as forecasted by the <i>d</i>-band center and -ICOHP (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">2h</a>). This comprehensive evaluation not only confirms the robustness of the cobalt-based PBA materials as efficient SIB electrodes but also highlights the predictive potential of electronic structural descriptors in guiding the design of high-performance energy storage materials.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423356-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6a1887ee-ed5e-46d8-86e3-03ee58413284/anie202423356-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6a1887ee-ed5e-46d8-86e3-03ee58413284/anie202423356-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e2859306-635b-4e70-9757-30b645db4c5c/anie202423356-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6a1887ee-ed5e-46d8-86e3-03ee58413284/anie202423356-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423356-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423356" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Structure and morphology. (a) Schematic for the coprecipitation synthesis of Co-based PBAs. TEM image and SEM element mapping images of (b) CoMn-PBA, (c) CoFe-PBA, (d) CoNi-PBA, (e) CoCu-PBA, and (f) CoZn-PBA. (g) The charging and discharging curves at 0.2 A/g, and (h) the comparison of the capacity retention rates of various Co-based PBAs. The Rietveld refinements of (i) CoV-PBA and (j) CoCo-PBA. (k) The XPS spectra of Co 2p for CoV-PBA and CoCo-PBA. (l) V 2p and N 1s of CoV-PBA. The (m) SEM image, (n) High-resolution scanning TEM (STEM) image, (o) SAED pattern, and (p) STEM-EDS elemental mappings of CoV-PBA.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The superior electrochemical performance of CoV-PBA is closely related to its finely tuned structural features. More detailed analyses have been conducted to understand the key factors influencing the electrochemical behavior of CoV-PBA further. The XRD Rietveld refinements of CoV-PBA and CoCo-PBA (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">2i</a>, <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">j</a> Table S3,4) indicate the presence of a phase-pure assigned to the cubic phase structure with a space group of <i>Fm-3 m</i>, possessing a good weighted profile R factor (<i>R<sub>wp</sub></i>=2.28 % and 1.63 %). The lattice parameter of CoV-PBA (a=10.16 Å) is lower than that of CoCo-PBA (a=10.12 Å), signifying that the amount of [Co(CN)<sub>6</sub>] defects is decreased and a more stable structure is obtained. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">2</a> (m, n) the well-resolved lattice fringes in the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images are examined with interplanar spacings of 0.25 and 0.20 nm corresponding to the (−440) and (442) planes of the CoV-PBA, respectively. The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) exhibits bright diffraction spots, further confirming the pure cubic structure of CoV-PBA (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">2o</a>). The element mapping results (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">2p</a>) further confirm the successful incorporation of V and the uniform element (Co, V, C, N) distributions within CoV-PBA, indicating the intact crystal structure and high purity. The stoichiometric composition of CoV-PBA and CoCo-PBA are identified as K<sub>0.05</sub>V<sub>0.99</sub>[Co(CN)<sub>6</sub>] and K<sub>0.23</sub>Co<sub>2.96</sub>[Co(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub>, determined from the results of the SEM-EDS analyses (Figure S12, 13) and ICP-OES results (Table S1). In the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA), a significant reduction in adsorbed (6.1 wt %) and interstitial water (4.5 %) is observed in CoV-PBA compared to CoCo-PBA (Figure S14), mitigating side reactions with organic electrolytes, ensuring stable cycling, and enhancing intrinsic safety for practical applications. The high-resolution XPS spectra (Figure S15) of V 2p (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">2l</a>) indicate corresponding trivalent and tetravalent species. The binding energy of the N 1s peak shifts from 707.8 eV to a higher value of 708.7 eV after the introduction of V, indicating that electron transfer occurs from N to V (π-donation). The Co 2p spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0002">2k</a>) display that the average valence states of Co cations increase (Co<sup>2+</sup> to Co<sup>3+</sup>) after the Co metal ion is substituted by V in the framework owing to the weakened π-back donation between Co and C, enhancing the specific capacity of the CoV-PBA anode material.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0041_R_d7751237e971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> This π-electron regulation at both ends of the −C≡N− bridge group leads to a synergistic modulation of the electronic structure, which is a critical factor for the significant improvement of electronic conductivity and structural stability.</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0003">3</a> shows the electrochemical performance of the CoV-PBA and CoCo-PBA. The CoV-PBA anode delivers a charge capacity of 622.7 mAh/g with an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 84 %, markedly surpassing CoCo-PBA, which achieves only 468 mAh/g with an ICE of 57 % (Figure S16). When cycled at 0.01–3 V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) in half cells, The CoV-PBA anode exhibits high discharge capacities of 637.8 and 570.5 mAh/g at 0.1 and 0.2 A/g, respectively, with an exceptional capacity retention of 100 % over 230 and 500 cycles, demonstrating exceptional durability and stability under different cycling conditions (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0003">3a</a> and Figure S17). As shown in Figure S18, the 100th and 200th GCD curves of the CoV-PBA anode fully overlapped, further implying a reversible and stable cycling performance. In contrast, the specific capacity of the CoCo-PBA anode deteriorates sharply with increasing charge–discharge cycles at 0.1 A/g. In Figure S19, CoV-PBA exhibits higher capacity at various rates than CoCo-PBA due to the improved Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and diffusion properties caused by electronic regulation between Co and V elements, whereby high reversible capacities of 632.7 to 174.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> are obtained under the current density of 0.05 and 30 A/g, respectively. Notably, the rate performance of the proposed CoV-PBA anode stands out as the highest among previously reported Co-based and V-based anodes for SIBs, as well as PBA-based anode for AMIBs (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0003">3b</a> and Table S4),<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0024_R_d7751237e993" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a>, <a href="#anie202423356-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0045_R_d7751237e993" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> which underscores the significant impact of synergistic modulation of the electronic structure through π-electron regulation of the −C≡N− bridge group. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0003">3c</a>, the CoV-PBA anode demonstrates a highly robust and reversible sodiumation/de-sodiumation process, presenting a reversible specific capacity of 395.4 mAh/g over 1000 cycles with an exceptionally low-capacity decay rate of merely 0.02 % at 1 A/g and retaining a high specific capacity of 280.3 mAh/g for 3000 cycles with high-capacity retention of 87 % at 5 A/g. Even tested at a high current density of 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the CoV-PBA anode demonstrates a long cycle life of 15000 cycles with only 0.0011 % degradation per cycle and 100 % CE (Figure S20). Furthermore, it is worth noting that the CoV-PBA anode transcends the other state-of-the-art anode materials under high rate (HR), ICE, cycle number, and capacity decay per cycle (CLPC) (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0003">3d</a> and Table S5), demonstrating its excellent overall electrochemical performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202423356-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0024_R_d7751237e1004" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a>, <a href="#anie202423356-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0045_R_d7751237e1004" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a>, <a href="#anie202423356-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202423356-bib-0054_R_d7751237e1004" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> Promisingly, the CoV-PBA anode exhibits high capacities (522 mA/g at 0.1 A/g) and excellent capacity retention (100 % over 100 cycles) under high areal loading of 6.11 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> (Figure S21). Additionally, it presents excellent low-temperature performance (−20 °C) (Figure S22) and commendable performance for lithium (LIB) and potassium ion batteries (PIB) (Figure S23).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423356-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5050693f-6bb8-4688-b389-d20f84cf4ce0/anie202423356-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5050693f-6bb8-4688-b389-d20f84cf4ce0/anie202423356-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1681efbd-ed3b-4d8b-b8d4-a44b91b2bf43/anie202423356-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5050693f-6bb8-4688-b389-d20f84cf4ce0/anie202423356-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423356-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423356" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance. (a) Cycling performance of CoV-PBA and CoCo-PBA anodes at a current density of 0.2 A/g. (b) The comparison of the rate capability of CoV-PBA anode with previously reported anodes. (c) Long-term cycling durability at 1 and 5 A/g of CoV-PBA anode. (d) Comparison results of electrochemical performances among CoV-PBA and other reported superior SIBs anodes and PBA-based anodes for AMIBs. (e) The fitted <i>b</i>-value of the redox peaks of CoV-PBA and CoCo-PBA anodes. Temperature-dependent Nyquist plots, Arrhenius curves, and the calculated activation energies for <i>R<sub>CT</sub></i> and <i>R<sub>SEI</sub></i> of (f) CoV-PBA, and (g) CoCo-PBA anodes. DRT analysis based on the <i>in situ</i> GEIS of (h) CoV-PBA, and (i) CoCo-PBA anodes.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>CV measurements at different scan rates of 0.1–0.5 mV/s were conducted to evaluate the kinetic difference between the CoV-PBA and CoCo-PBA anodes (Figure S24). Notably, the redox peaks of CoV-PBA at the various scan rates are similar, indicating the high reaction reversibility of the CoV-PBA electrode. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0003">3e</a>, the fitted <i>b-value</i> of the <i>log(i)</i> vs <i>log(v)</i> plots for R<sub>1</sub>, R<sub>2</sub>, O<sub>3</sub>, O<sub>2</sub>, and O<sub>1</sub> peaks of the CoCo-PBA anode are 0.37, 0.66, 0.52, 0.34, and 0.69, respectively, which is close to 0.5. This indicates a diffusion-controlled reaction process with slow Na diffusion kinetics impacted by factors such as low sodium ion adsorption energy, limited electronic conductivity, and high Na-ion diffusion energy barriers of the CoCo-PBA anode. In contrast, the fitted <i>b-value</i> for R<sub>1</sub>, R<sub>2</sub>, O<sub>3</sub>, O<sub>2</sub>, and O<sub>1</sub> peaks of the CoV-PBA peaks are 0.97, 0.95, 0.973, 0.76, and 0.68, respectively, which is higher than that of the CoCo-PBA anode and close to 1 for O<sub>1</sub>, O<sub>2</sub>, and O<sub>3</sub>. Additionally, the CoV-PBA anode displays stronger pseudocapacitive behavior than the CoCo-PBA under the different scan rates (Figure S25, 26). It is found that CoV-PBA possesses faster reaction kinetics than that of the CoCo-PBA anode, which can be ascribed to the higher electronic conductivity and moderate Na-ion adsorption and diffusion energy of the CoV-PBA anode. As shown in the <i>in situ</i> EIS results (Figure S27) simulated by the DRT technology (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0003">3h</a>, <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0003">i</a>), the peak at −5.5 ~ −4.5, −4.5–3, −3 ~ −1, and -1–2 can correspond to internal resistance (<i>R<sub>0</sub></i>), the ionic transport at the interfacial SEI (<i>R<sub>SEI</sub></i>), charge transfer in electrodes (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and solid-state diffusion (<i>W<sub>diff</sub></i>), respectively. The <i>R<sub>0</sub></i> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the CoV-PBA electrode are smaller than that of CoCo-PBA during the whole charge and discharge process, indicating the higher electronic conductivity and faster electrochemical reaction kinetic of the CoV-PBA anode, which is consistent with the DFT result. Furthermore, the <i>W<sub>diff</sub></i> resistance of the CoV-PBA electrode at the end of the charge and process is smaller than that of CoCo-PBA, suggesting an excellent ionic transport rate of the CoV-PBA anode, which is coincident with the GITT and DFT results. It can be seen that despite the anticipated SEI layer formation, the <i>R<sub>SEI</sub></i> of the CoV-PBA anode experiences no substantial increase during the first cycle, which is consistent with its high ICE and considerable SEI stability. Notably, both the activation energy barriers of charge transfer (<i>E<sub>a1</sub></i>) and transport across the SEI film (<i>E<sub>a2</sub></i>) of CoV-PBA anode (<i>E<sub>a1</sub></i>=11.88 kJ/mol, <i>E<sub>a2</sub></i>=31.5 kJ/mol) have been significantly reduced as compared to those of CoCo-PBA anode (<i>E<sub>a1</sub></i>=46.39 kJ/mol, <i>E<sub>a2</sub></i>=60.19 kJ/mol), strongly indicating that the higher interfacial Na<sup>+</sup>diffusion kinetics of CoV-PBA anode (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0003">3f</a>, 3 g). Moreover, the resistance of the anode in CoV-PBA is lower than that in CoCo-PBA at the same cycling number, further confirming the superior ionic transport rate and interfacial stability of CoV-PBA anode (Figure S28). Therefore, these results demonstrate that π-electron regulation with moderate Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption ability and diffusion barrier enables CoV-PBA with fast reaction kinetics of sodiumation/de-sodiumation.</p>
         
         <p><i>In situ</i> XRD and Raman, <i>ex situ</i> XPS and FTIR were further conducted to study the phase transitions during Na-ions insertion and extraction in the CoV-PBA anode. In the initial stage of the discharging process, the peaks at 17.8 and 35.5° are gradually shifted to higher angles with the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>, indicating a phase transformation from a cubic phase to a sodium-rich rhombohedral phase (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0004">4a</a> and S29). Upon continued discharge, the XRD peaks progressively diminished in intensity, indicating that the further reduction of metal ions induces the CoV-PBA anode to transition into an amorphous state, whose isotropic nature enables uniform volume changes and effectively adapts to volumetric strain at the late stage of discharge, thereby improving cyclic stability. Upon further deep discharge, a low-intensity characteristic peak appears at approximately 41.5° which can be indexed the (110) plane of V (JCPDS 22–1058), indicating the formation of low crystallinity V at the late discharge state. This result is consistent with those of the results obtained by <i>ex situ</i> TEM (Figure S29b, c). Notably, the peaks experience reversible movements and a transition from monoclinic to cubic phase during the charging process, demonstrating good electrochemical reversibility during sodiation/de-sodiation of the CoV-PBA anode. This phenomenon is further verified by <i>in situ</i> Raman spectroscopy, wherein the peaks of M–CN at the range of 2000–2200 cm<sup>−1</sup> experience reversible evolution (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0004">4b</a>). This behavior starkly contrasts with the reported behavior of PBAs as anode materials for Alkali Metal Ion Batteries (AMIBs), where the crystalline structure undergoes an irreversible crystal-to-amorphous transformation. As shown in the <i>ex situ</i> XPS results (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0004">4c</a> and S30), the characteristic peak of the Co<sup>3+</sup> located at 781.4 eV gradually weakens until it disappears, and two newborn peaks emerge at 782.1 eV (Co<sup>2+</sup>) and 780.4 eV (Co<sup>+</sup>) in the discharging process. Upon charging, the reverse process occurs, suggesting the gradual recovery of the Co<sup>3+</sup> with the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>. Besides, it can be found from the V 2<i>p</i> spectrum that the presence of V<sup>0</sup>, V<sup>+</sup>, and V<sup>2+</sup> at 0.01 V, while V<sup>3+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup> can be observed at 3.0 V, indicating the good redox reversibility of V components throughout the entire charge and discharge processes. The reversible shift of the N 1s peak proves that N functions as the active site for Na<sup>+</sup> acceptance, aligning with the <i>ex situ</i> FTIR results (Figure S31). The above-mentioned multi-electron redox chemistry of the bimetallic active centers (Co and V) enhances the extent of redox reactions and supports the higher specific capacity of the obtained CoV-PBA (Figure S29c).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423356-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fc186e37-739c-4be3-b6ca-0bebeb88bd0e/anie202423356-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fc186e37-739c-4be3-b6ca-0bebeb88bd0e/anie202423356-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/60dd0377-801a-4cdf-8760-6405a2bce70b/anie202423356-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fc186e37-739c-4be3-b6ca-0bebeb88bd0e/anie202423356-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </div>

               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>COHP analysis has been further carried out to evaluate the bonding stability of the sodiation state. As displayed in the V−N COHP profiles of the CoV-PBA, the high bonding participation during the entire sodiation process reflects the strong V−N interactions (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0004">4d</a>). The change in -ICOHP values of the CoV-PBA (5 %) anode before and after Na intercalation is significantly lower than that of CoCo-PBA (19 %) (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0004">4e</a>, <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0004">f</a>). Besides, as shown in the <i>in situ</i> optical microscopic images, the CoV-PBA electrode can achieve a low volume expansion rate of 116 % during the charge and discharge processes (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0004">4g</a>). This demonstrates that the π-donation between V and N enhances the V−N bond strength, which can reduce volumetric expansion during the cycle process, thereby improving the stability and electrochemical reversibility of the CoV−PBA anode. The XRD patterns and SEM elemental mapping of CoV-PBA electrodes after cycling reveal a slight reduction in crystallinity, while preserving the characteristic PBA structure and uniform distribution of Co and V, further confirming their excellent electrochemical reversibility and stability (Figure S32, S33). In addition, gas evolution detection experiments demonstrate that the CoV-PBA electrodes exhibit exceptional operational reliability and safety (Figure S34). Notably, the <i>ex situ</i> SEM images (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0004">4h</a>) indicate that the nanocubes converted to a porous structure consisting of interconnected nanoparticles as the cycle proceeds. This favorable morphology evolution (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0004">4i</a>) is beneficial for relieving mechanical strain−stress as well as accelerating Na-ion diffusion during long-term cycling, which will contribute to improving electrochemical performances.</p>
         
         <p>To further evaluate the application potential of CoV-PBA anode material for SIBs, the full cells were assembled coupling the CoV-PBA anode with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) and manganese hexacyanoferrate (MnHCF) cathodes (Figure S35–37). The assembled NVP||CoV-PBA and MnHCF||CoV-PBA can enable remarkable energy densities of 257 and 336 Wh/kg, respectively (calculated based on the total mass of the active materials of the anode and cathode). The full cell in this work exhibits competitive performance compared with the state-of-the-art sodium-ion full cells that have been previously reported (Figure S37f and Table S6). In addition, the NVP||CoV-PBA full cell in Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0005">5a</a> demonstrates exceptional performance, delivering capacities of 510.9, 422.7, 303.2, 73.2, and 68.1 mAh/g under current densities of 0.1, 0.5, 1, 10, and 20 A/g, respectively. Furthermore, the SIB full cell exhibits a consistent capacity of 255 mAh/g after 400 cycles at 1 A/g with a sustained capacity retention of 86.3 % (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0005">5b</a>). Leveraging these impressive electrochemical performances, the corresponding coin cell and pouch cell can power a light board and fan, respectively (insert in Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0005">5b</a>, Figure S38, Video S1, 2), unequivocally showcasing its practical viability. These exceptional properties underscore the potential of CoV-PBA as a highly efficient anode material for SIBs.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202423356-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/28706edd-df34-400d-bff1-bae19d3ba976/anie202423356-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/28706edd-df34-400d-bff1-bae19d3ba976/anie202423356-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9fc7daef-996e-49b1-8a5d-b28534dbbecc/anie202423356-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/28706edd-df34-400d-bff1-bae19d3ba976/anie202423356-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202423356-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202423356" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Full-cell performance of CoV-PBA and the SIBs electrochemical performance of other Co-based and CoV-based anodes. (a) Rate performance, and (b) long-cycle performance of NVP//CoV-PBA full cell, and inserted optical image displays an LED board and fan powered by one as-developed coin cell and pouch cell, respectively. (c) Charge−discharge curves, (b) <i>p</i>-band center and specific capacity, and (e) <i>d</i>-band center and capacity retention rate of Co−M (V, Mn, Fe, Ni, Cu, Zn) selenide anodes. (f) The comparisons of the SIBs performance between Co-based and CoV-based anodes (oxide, phosphide, sulfide). (g) Schematic representation of the <i>d-p</i> regulation of Co-based and CoV-based anodes.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further expand Co-based PBA materials and verify the feasibility of using “<i>p</i>-band center, <i>d</i>-band center” as descriptors to predict the sodium storage performance, we have also explored the controllable synthesis of Co−M PBA derivatives such as oxide, phosphide, sulfide, and selenide as anodes for SIBs. Their corresponding DOS, XRD patterns, SEM, and elements mapping images are presented in Figure S39–46. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0005">5</a> (c, d), the (Co<sub>0.5</sub>V<sub>0.5</sub>)Se<sub>2</sub> delivers a moderate <i>p</i>-band center and the highest specific capacity (674 mAh/g) among all seven Co-based selenide ((Co<sub>0.5</sub>M<sub>0.5</sub>)Se<sub>2</sub>) anodes, indicating that metal elements regulate reaction kinetics and thus promote Na<sup>+</sup> storage. Surprisingly, the experimental stability results of (Co<sub>0.5</sub>M<sub>0.5</sub>)Se<sub>2</sub> are well consistent with the <i>d</i>-band center value (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0005">5e</a> and S43), i.e., the positive correlation between durability and <i>d</i>-band center, which means that a higher <i>d</i>-band center could lead to more stable SIBs anode materials. These results underscore the applicability of the <i>p</i>-band center and <i>d</i>-band center as predictive descriptors for the specific capacity and stability of anode materials with similar theoretical capacities. In addition, the sodium storage performance of the CoV-based anode (oxide, phosphide, sulfide) is much better than Co-based anode (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0005">5f</a> and S44–46), indicating that the V elements with vacant orbitals can form stronger π-donation interactions with non-metal ions, thereby, enhancing internal charge transfer within the material. This redistribution of electrons results in CoV-based anode material featuring a higher <i>d</i>-band center and an optimal <i>p</i>-band center. These characteristics significantly improve the structural stability and expedite the sodium-ion redox kinetics of the material, culminating in a highly stable and high-rate anode material (Figure <a href="#anie202423356-fig-0005">5g</a>).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423356-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202423356-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, seven Co-based PBA samples were synthesized using a low-energy consumption co-precipitation method. The experimental results corroborated the DFT predictions, showing that a moderate <i>p</i>-band center with balanced <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>E<sub>d</sub></i> is highly favorable for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and diffusion. Furthermore, the <i>d</i>-band center of metal cations and the integrated crystal orbital Hamilton population (ICOHP) of the TM−N band correlate linearly with the stability among the Co-based PBA samples, with CoV-PBAs exhibiting the best sodium-ion adsorption/diffusion kinetics and cycling performance. <i>In situ</i>/<i>ex situ</i> techniques demonstrated that π-electron regulation by V introduction synergistically modulates the electronic structure, enhancing the reversibility and kinetics of redox reactions. The CoV-PBA (CV) anode exhibited remarkable sodium-ion storage performance, with a high specific capacity (~565 mAh/g at 0.1 A/g), excellent cycling stability (over 15,000 cycles with ~97.7 % capacity retention), and superior rate capability (~174.7 mAh/g at 30 A/g). Additionally, the study verified that the “<i>p</i>-band center” and “<i>d</i>-band center” serve as effective descriptors for quantifying the capacity and stability of other bimetal Co-based anodes (oxides, phosphides, sulfides, selenides) with similar theoretical capacities. This discovery can reduce the number of relevant parameters despite the complexity of anode material structures, significantly expanding the screening of anode material selections for practical SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202423356-sec-0004">
